<p>The <b>Sahara</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ær/: 'arr' in 'marry'">ær</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African continent</a>. With an area of 9,200,000 square kilometres (3,600,000 sq mi), it is the largest hot desert in the world and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_deserts_by_area" title="List of deserts by area">third-largest desert</a> overall, smaller only than the deserts of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and the northern <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geology.com_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-geology.com-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Size_and_Population_of_Sahara_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Size_and_Population_of_Sahara-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name "Sahara" is derived from the Arabic word for "desert" in the feminine irregular form, the singular <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣaḥra'</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صحراء</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">/ˈsˤaħra/</a></span>), plural <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣaḥārā</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صَحَارَى</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">/ˈsˤaħaːraː/</a></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣaḥār</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صَحَار</span></span>), <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣaḥrāwāt</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صَحْرَاوَات</span></span>), <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣaḥāriy</i></span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صَحَارِي</span></span>).
</p><p>The desert comprises much of <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, excluding the fertile region on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> coast, the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile Valley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>. It stretches from the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> in the east and the Mediterranean in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> in the west, where the landscape gradually changes from desert to coastal plains. To the south it is bounded by the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>, a belt of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_grasslands,_savannas,_and_shrublands" title="Tropical and subtropical grasslands, savannas, and shrublands">semi-arid tropical savanna</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> valley and the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_(region)" title="Sudan (region)">Sudan region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>. The Sahara can be divided into several regions, including the western Sahara, the central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahaggar_Mountains" title="Ahaggar Mountains">Ahaggar Mountains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tibesti_Mountains" title="Tibesti Mountains">Tibesti Mountains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains" title="Aïr Mountains">Aïr Mountains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9n%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Ténéré">Ténéré</a> desert, and the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Desert" title="Libyan Desert">Libyan Desert</a>.
</p><p>For several hundred thousand years, the Sahara has alternated between desert and savanna grassland in a 20,000-year cycle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> caused by the <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt#Earth" title="Axial tilt">axis</a> as it rotates around the Sun, which changes the location of the <a href="/wiki/North_African_climate_cycles" title="North African climate cycles">North African monsoon</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vegetation_Africa.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1165" data-file-width="1295" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vegetation_Africa.png/290px-Vegetation_Africa.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vegetation_Africa.png/435px-Vegetation_Africa.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vegetation_Africa.png/580px-Vegetation_Africa.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vegetation_Africa.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main biomes in Africa</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4168" data-file-width="7432" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg/220px-Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg/330px-Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg/440px-Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sahara_satellite_hires.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A satellite image of the Sahara by <a href="/wiki/NASA_WorldWind" title="NASA WorldWind">NASA WorldWind</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Sahara covers large parts of <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. It covers 9 million square kilometres (3,500,000 sq mi), amounting to 31% of Africa. If all areas with a mean annual precipitation of less than 250 mm were included, the Sahara would be 11 million square kilometres (4,200,000 sq mi). It is one of three distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiographic_provinces" title="Physiographic provinces">physiographic provinces</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Physiographic_regions_of_the_world#Compendium_table_of_the_worlds_physiographic_regions" title="Physiographic regions of the world">African massive physiographic division</a>. Sahara is so large and bright that, in theory, it could be detected from other stars as a surface feature of Earth, with near-current technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sahara is mainly rocky <a href="/wiki/Hamada" title="Hamada">hamada</a> (stone plateaus); <a href="/wiki/Erg_(landform)" title="Erg (landform)">ergs</a> (sand seas – large areas covered with sand <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dunes</a>) form only a minor part, but many of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sand_dune" title="Sand dune">sand dunes</a> are over 180 metres (590 ft) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">Wind</a> or rare rainfall shape the desert features: sand dunes, dune fields, sand seas, stone plateaus, gravel plains (<i>reg</i>), <a href="/wiki/Wadi" title="Wadi">dry valleys</a> (<i>wadi</i>), <a href="/wiki/Dry_lake" title="Dry lake">dry lakes</a> (<i>oued</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Salt_pan_(geology)" title="Salt pan (geology)">salt flats</a> (<i>shatt</i> or <i>chott</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA1327_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA1327-11">[11]</a></sup> Unusual landforms include the <a href="/wiki/Richat_Structure" title="Richat Structure">Richat Structure</a> in Mauritania.
</p><p>Several deeply dissected mountains, many volcanic, rise from the desert, including the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains" title="Aïr Mountains">Aïr Mountains</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahaggar_Mountains" title="Ahaggar Mountains">Ahaggar Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saharan_Atlas" title="Saharan Atlas">Saharan Atlas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibesti_Mountains" title="Tibesti Mountains">Tibesti Mountains</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adrar_des_Iforas" title="Adrar des Iforas">Adrar des Iforas</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Sea_Hills" title="Red Sea Hills">Red Sea Hills</a>. The highest peak in the Sahara is <a href="/wiki/Emi_Koussi" title="Emi Koussi">Emi Koussi</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Shield_volcano" title="Shield volcano">shield volcano</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibesti" title="Tibesti">Tibesti</a> range of northern Chad.
</p><p>The central Sahara is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperarid" title="Hyperarid">hyperarid</a>, with sparse vegetation. The northern and southern reaches of the desert, along with the highlands, have areas of sparse grassland and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desert_shrub" title="Desert shrub">desert shrub</a>, with trees and taller shrubs in <a href="/wiki/Wadi" title="Wadi">wadis</a>, where moisture collects. In the central, hyperarid region, there are many subdivisions of the great desert: <a href="/wiki/Tanezrouft" title="Tanezrouft">Tanezrouft</a>, the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9n%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Ténéré">Ténéré</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Desert" title="Libyan Desert">Libyan Desert</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Desert" title="Eastern Desert">Eastern Desert</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Desert" title="Nubian Desert">Nubian Desert</a> and others. These extremely arid areas often receive no rain for years.
</p><p>To the north, the Sahara skirts the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> in Egypt and portions of Libya, but in <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, the Sahara borders the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_forest,_woodland,_and_scrub" title="Mediterranean forest, woodland, and scrub">Mediterranean forest, woodland, and scrub</a> eco-regions of northern Africa, all of which have a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a> characterized by hot summers and cool and rainy winters. According to the botanical criteria of <a href="/wiki/Frank_White_(botanist)" title="Frank White (botanist)">Frank White</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and geographer Robert Capot-Rey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove1958_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grove1958-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bisson2003_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bisson2003-14">[14]</a></sup> the northern limit of the Sahara corresponds to the northern limit of <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> cultivation and the southern limit of the range of <a href="/wiki/Esparto" title="Esparto">esparto</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grass</a> typical of the Mediterranean climate portion of the Maghreb and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">Iberia</a>. The northern limit also corresponds to the 100 mm (3.9 in) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotherm_(contour_line)" title="Isotherm (contour line)">isohyet</a> of annual precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton2007_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walton2007-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the south, the Sahara is bounded by the Sahel, a belt of dry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_savanna" title="Tropical savanna">tropical savanna</a> with a summer rainy season that extends across Africa from east to west. The southern limit of the Sahara is indicated botanically by the southern limit of <i><a href="/wiki/Cornulaca_monacantha" title="Cornulaca monacantha">Cornulaca monacantha</a></i> (a drought-tolerant member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chenopodiaceae" title="Chenopodiaceae">Chenopodiaceae</a>), or northern limit of <i><a href="/wiki/Cenchrus_biflorus" title="Cenchrus biflorus">Cenchrus biflorus</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grass</a> typical of the Sahel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove1958_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grove1958-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bisson2003_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bisson2003-14">[14]</a></sup> According to climatic criteria, the southern limit of the Sahara corresponds to the 150 mm (5.9 in) isohyet of annual precipitation (this is a long-term average, since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> varies annually).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton2007_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walton2007-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important cities located in the Sahara include <a href="/wiki/Nouakchott" title="Nouakchott">Nouakchott</a>, the capital of Mauritania; <a href="/wiki/Tamanrasset" title="Tamanrasset">Tamanrasset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ouargla" title="Ouargla">Ouargla</a>, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9char" title="Béchar">Béchar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hassi_Messaoud" title="Hassi Messaoud">Hassi Messaoud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gharda%C3%AFa" title="Ghardaïa">Ghardaïa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/El_Oued" title="El Oued">El Oued</a> in Algeria; <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a> in Mali; <a href="/wiki/Agadez" title="Agadez">Agadez</a> in Niger; <a href="/wiki/Ghat,_Libya" title="Ghat, Libya">Ghat</a> in Libya; and <a href="/wiki/Faya-Largeau" title="Faya-Largeau">Faya-Largeau</a> in Chad.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hoggar8.jpg/220px-Hoggar8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Hoggar8.jpg/330px-Hoggar8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Hoggar8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oasis</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahaggar_Mountains" title="Ahaggar Mountains">Ahaggar Mountains</a>. Oases support some life forms in extremely arid deserts.</div></div></div>
<p>The Sahara is the world's largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_desert" title="Hot desert">hot desert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It is located in the <a href="/wiki/Horse_latitudes" title="Horse latitudes">horse latitudes</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">subtropical ridge</a>, a significant belt of semi-permanent subtropical warm-core high pressure where the air from the upper troposphere usually descends, warming and drying the lower troposphere and preventing cloud formation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The permanent absence of clouds allows unhindered light and thermal radiation. The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_instability#Stable_atmosphere" title="Atmospheric instability">stability of the atmosphere</a> above the desert prevents any convective overturning, thus making rainfall virtually non-existent. As a consequence, the weather tends to be sunny, dry and stable with a minimal chance of rainfall. Subsiding, diverging, dry air masses associated with subtropical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-pressure_system" title="High-pressure system">high-pressure systems</a> are extremely unfavorable for the development of convectional showers. The subtropical ridge is the predominant factor that explains the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_desert_climate" title="Hot desert climate">hot desert climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>BWh</i>) of this vast region. The descending airflow is the strongest and the most effective over the eastern part of the Great Desert, in the Libyan Desert: this is the sunniest, driest and the most nearly "rainless" place on the planet, rivaling the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Desert" title="Atacama Desert">Atacama Desert</a>, lying in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>.
</p><p>The rainfall inhibition and the dissipation of cloud cover are most accentuated over the eastern section of the Sahara rather than the western. The prevailing <a href="/wiki/Air_mass" title="Air mass">air mass</a> lying above the Sahara is the continental tropical (cT) air mass, which is hot and dry. Hot, dry air masses primarily form over the North-African desert from the heating of the vast continental land area, and it affects the whole desert during most of the year. Because of this <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">extreme heating</a> process, a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_low" title="Thermal low">thermal low</a> is usually noticed near the surface, and is the strongest and the most developed during the summertime. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sahara_High&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sahara High (page does not exist)">Sahara High</a> represents the eastern continental extension of the <a href="/wiki/Azores_High" title="Azores High">Azores High</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> centered over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Ocean" title="North Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic Ocean</a>. The subsidence of the Sahara High nearly reaches the ground during the coolest part of the year, while it is confined to the upper troposphere during the hottest periods.
</p><p>The effects of local surface low pressure are extremely limited because upper-level subsidence still continues to block any form of air ascent. Also, to be protected against rain-bearing weather systems by the atmospheric circulation itself, the desert is made even drier by its geographical configuration and location. Indeed, the extreme aridity of the Sahara is not only explained by the subtropical high pressure: the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> of Algeria, Morocco and Tunisia also help to enhance the aridity of the northern part of the desert. These major mountain ranges act as a barrier, causing a strong <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> effect on the leeward side by dropping much of the humidity brought by atmospheric disturbances along the <a href="/wiki/Polar_front" title="Polar front">polar front</a> which affects the surrounding Mediterranean climates.
</p><p>The primary source of rain in the Sahara is the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>, a continuous belt of low-pressure systems near the equator which bring the brief, short and irregular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainy_season" title="Rainy season">rainy season</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> and southern Sahara. Rainfall in this giant desert has to overcome the physical and atmospheric barriers that normally prevent the production of precipitation. The harsh climate of the Sahara is characterized by: extremely low, unreliable, highly erratic rainfall; extremely high sunshine duration values; high temperatures year-round; negligible rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a>; a significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_temperature_variation" title="Diurnal temperature variation">diurnal temperature variation</a>; and extremely high levels of <a href="/wiki/Potential_evaporation" title="Potential evaporation">potential evaporation</a> which are the highest recorded worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span></h3><p>
The sky is usually clear above the desert, and the <a href="/wiki/Sunshine_duration" title="Sunshine duration">sunshine duration</a> is extremely high everywhere in the Sahara. Most of the desert has more than 3,600 hours of bright sunshine per year (over 82% of daylight hours), and a wide area in the eastern part has over 4,000 hours of bright sunshine per year (over 91% of daylight hours). The highest values are very close to the theoretical maximum value. A value of 4300 hours (98%) of the time would be<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> recorded in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a>) and in the Nubian Desert (<a href="/wiki/Wadi_Halfa" title="Wadi Halfa">Wadi Halfa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.fr_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.fr-18">[18]</a></sup> The annual average <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">direct solar irradiation</a> is around 2,800 kWh/(m<sup>2</sup> year) in the Great Desert. The Sahara has a huge potential for solar energy production.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg/220px-Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg/330px-Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg/440px-Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Algeria_Sahara_Desert_Photo_From_Drone_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sand_dunes" title="Sand dunes">Sand dunes</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algerian</a> Sahara</div></div></div>
<p>The high position of the Sun, the extremely low relative humidity, and the lack of <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a> and rainfall make the Great Desert the hottest large region in the world, and the hottest place on Earth during summer in some spots. The average high temperature exceeds 38 to 40 °C or 100.4 to 104.0 °F during the hottest month nearly everywhere in the desert except at very high altitudes. The world's highest officially recorded average daily high temperature<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Average over what time period? (April 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> was 47 °C or 116.6 °F in a remote desert town in the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_Desert" title="Algerian Desert">Algerian Desert</a> called <a href="/wiki/Bou_Bernous" title="Bou Bernous">Bou Bernous</a>, at an elevation of 378 metres (1,240 ft) above sea level,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.fr_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.fr-18">[18]</a></sup> and only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_Valley,_California" title="Death Valley, California">Death Valley, California</a> rivals it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Other hot spots in Algeria such as <a href="/wiki/Adrar,_Algeria" title="Adrar, Algeria">Adrar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timimoun" title="Timimoun">Timimoun</a>, <a href="/wiki/In_Salah" title="In Salah">In Salah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ouallene" title="Ouallene">Ouallene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aoulef" title="Aoulef">Aoulef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reggane" title="Reggane">Reggane</a> with an elevation between 200 and 400 metres (660 and 1,310 ft) above sea level get slightly lower summer average highs, around 46 °C or 114.8 °F during the hottest months of the year. Salah, well known in Algeria for its extreme heat, has average high temperatures of 43.8 °C or 110.8 °F, 46.4 °C or 115.5 °F, 45.5 °C or 113.9 °F and 41.9 °C or 107.4 °F in June, July, August and September respectively. There are even hotter spots in the Sahara, but they are located in extremely remote areas, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Azalai" title="Azalai">Azalai</a>, lying in northern Mali. The major part of the desert experiences around three to five months when the average high strictly<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> exceeds 40 °C or 104 °F; while in the southern central part of the desert, there are up to six or seven months when the average high temperature strictly<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> exceeds 40 °C or 104 °F. Some examples of this are <a href="/wiki/Bilma" title="Bilma">Bilma</a>, Niger and Faya-Largeau, Chad. The annual average daily temperature exceeds 20 °C or 68 °F everywhere and can approach 30 °C or 86 °F in the hottest regions year-round. However, most of the desert has a value in excess of 25 °C or 77 °F.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg/220px-Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg/330px-Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg/440px-Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunset_in_Sahara.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sunset in Sahara</div></div></div>
<p>Sand and ground temperatures are even more extreme. During daytime, the sand temperature is extremely high: it can easily reach 80 °C or 176 °F or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-20">[20]</a></sup> A sand temperature of 83.5 °C (182.3 °F) has been recorded in <a href="/wiki/Port_Sudan" title="Port Sudan">Port Sudan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-20">[20]</a></sup> Ground temperatures of 72 °C or 161.6 °F have been recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Adrar_Plateau" title="Adrar Plateau">Adrar of Mauritania</a> and a value of 75 °C (167 °F) has been measured in <a href="/wiki/Borkou" title="Borkou">Borkou</a>, northern Chad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to lack of cloud cover and very low humidity, the desert usually has high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_temperature_variation" title="Diurnal temperature variation">diurnal temperature variations</a> between days and nights. However, it is a myth that the nights are especially cold after extremely hot days in the Sahara.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On average, nighttime temperatures tend to be 13–20 °C (23–36 °F) cooler than in the daytime. The smallest variations are found along the coastal regions due to high humidity and are often even lower than a 10 °C or 18 °F difference, while the largest variations are found in inland desert areas where the humidity is the lowest, mainly in the southern Sahara. Still, it is true that winter nights can be cold, as it can drop to the freezing point and even below, especially in high-elevation areas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="perhaps provide some examples of specific nighttime lows here? (February 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The frequency of subfreezing winter nights in the Sahara is strongly influenced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Oscillation" title="North Atlantic Oscillation">North Atlantic Oscillation</a> (NAO), with warmer winter temperatures during negative NAO events and cooler winters with more frosts when the NAO is positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This is because the weaker clockwise flow around the eastern side of the subtropical anticyclone during negative NAO winters, although too dry to produce more than negligible precipitation, does reduce the flow of dry, cold air from higher latitudes of Eurasia into the Sahara significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation">Precipitation</span></h3>
<p>The average annual rainfall ranges from very low in the northern and southern fringes of the desert to nearly non-existent over the central and the eastern part. The thin northern fringe of the desert receives more winter cloudiness and rainfall due to the arrival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_pressure_system" title="Low pressure system">low pressure systems</a> over the Mediterranean Sea along the polar front, although very attenuated by the rain shadow effects of the mountains and the annual average rainfall ranges from 100 millimetres (4 in) to 250 millimetres (10 in). For example, <a href="/wiki/Biskra" title="Biskra">Biskra</a>, Algeria, and <a href="/wiki/Ouarzazate" title="Ouarzazate">Ouarzazate</a>, Morocco, are found in this zone. The southern fringe of the desert along the border with the Sahel receives summer cloudiness and rainfall due to the arrival of the Intertropical Convergence Zone from the south and the annual average rainfall ranges from 100 millimetres (4 in) to 250 millimetres (10 in). For example, Timbuktu, Mali and <a href="/wiki/Agadez" title="Agadez">Agadez</a>, Niger are found in this zone. The vast central hyper-arid core of the desert is virtually never affected by northerly or southerly atmospheric disturbances and permanently remains under the influence of the strongest anticyclonic weather regime, and the annual average rainfall can drop to less than 1 millimetre (0.04 in). In fact, most of the Sahara receives less than 20 millimetres (0.8 in). Of the 9,000,000 square kilometres (3,500,000 sq mi) of desert land in the Sahara, an area of about 2,800,000 square kilometres (1,100,000 sq mi) (about 31% of the total area) receives an annual average rainfall amount of 10 millimetres (0.4 in) or less, while some 1,500,000 square kilometres (580,000 sq mi) (about 17% of the total area) receives an average of 5 millimetres (0.2 in) or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> The annual average rainfall is virtually zero over a wide area of some 1,000,000 square kilometres (390,000 sq mi) in the eastern Sahara comprising deserts of: Libya, Egypt and Sudan (<a href="/wiki/Tazirbu" title="Tazirbu">Tazirbu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kufra" title="Kufra">Kufra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dakhla_Oasis" title="Dakhla Oasis">Dakhla</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharga" title="Kharga">Kharga</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farafra" title="Farafra">Farafra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asyut" title="Asyut">Asyut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sohag" title="Sohag">Sohag</a>, Luxor, Aswan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel_temples" title="Abu Simbel temples">Abu Simbel</a>, Wadi Halfa) where the long-term mean approximates 0.5 millimetres (0.02 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> Rainfall is very unreliable and erratic in the Sahara as it may vary considerably year by year. In full contrast to the negligible annual rainfall amounts, the annual rates of potential evaporation are extraordinarily high, roughly ranging from 2,500 millimetres (100 in) per year to more than 6,000 millimetres (240 in) per year in the whole desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Nowhere else on Earth has air been found as dry and evaporative as in the Sahara region. However, at least two instances of snowfall have been recorded in Sahara, in February 1979 and December 2016, both in the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ain_Sefra" title="Ain Sefra">Ain Sefra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Desertification_and_prehistoric_climate">Desertification and prehistoric climate<span class="anchor" id="Climate_history"></span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_humid_period" title="African humid period">African humid period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_North_Africa" title="Prehistoric North Africa">Prehistoric North Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_African_climate_cycles" title="North African climate cycles">North African climate cycles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3950" data-file-width="4156" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png/220px-Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png/330px-Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png/440px-Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vegetation and water bodies in the <a href="/wiki/Eemian" title="Eemian">Eemian</a> (bottom) and <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> (top)</div></div></div>
<p>One theory for the formation of the Sahara is that the monsoon in Northern Africa was weakened because of glaciation during the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> period, starting two or three million years ago. Another theory is that the monsoon was weakened when the ancient <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Sea</a> dried up during the <a href="/wiki/Tortonian" title="Tortonian">Tortonian</a> period around 7 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The climate of the Sahara has undergone enormous variations between wet and dry over the last few hundred thousand years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes-27">[27]</a></sup> believed to be caused by long-term changes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African_climate_cycle" title="North African climate cycle">North African climate cycle</a> that alters the path of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African_Monsoon" title="North African Monsoon">North African Monsoon</a> – usually southward. The cycle is caused by a 41,000-year cycle in which the tilt of the earth changes between 22° and 24.5°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> At present (2000), we are in a dry period, but it is expected that the Sahara will become green again in 15,000 years. When the North African monsoon is at its strongest annual precipitation and subsequent vegetation in the Sahara region increase, resulting in conditions commonly referred to as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Sahara" title="Green Sahara">green Sahara</a>". For a relatively weak North African monsoon, the opposite is true, with decreased annual precipitation and less vegetation resulting in a phase of the Sahara climate cycle known as the "desert Sahara".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foley1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foley1-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The idea that changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a> (solar heating) caused by long-term changes in Earth's orbit are a controlling factor for the long-term variations in the strength of monsoon patterns across the globe was first suggested by Rudolf Spitaler in the late nineteenth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruddiman3_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruddiman3-29">[29]</a></sup> The hypothesis was later formally proposed and tested by the meteorologist John Kutzbach in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kutzbach4_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kutzbach4-30">[30]</a></sup> Kutzbach's ideas about the impacts of insolation on global monsoonal patterns have become widely accepted today as the underlying driver of long-term monsoonal cycles. Kutzbach never formally named his hypothesis and as such it is referred to here as the "<a href="/wiki/North_African_climate_cycles#Orbital_Monsoon_Hypothesis" title="North African climate cycles">Orbital Monsoon Hypothesis</a>" as suggested by Ruddiman in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruddiman3_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruddiman3-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg/220px-Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg/330px-Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg/440px-Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Village_Telly_in_Mali.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region of Mali</div></div></div>
<p>During the last <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial period</a>, the Sahara was much larger than it is today, extending south beyond its current boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The end of the glacial period brought more rain to the Sahara, from about 8000 BCE to 6000 BCE, perhaps because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_pressure_area" title="Low pressure area">low pressure areas</a> over the collapsing <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a> to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Once the ice sheets were gone, the northern Sahara dried out. In the southern Sahara, the drying trend was initially counteracted by the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>, which brought rain further north than it does today. By around 4200 BCE, however, the monsoon retreated south to approximately where it is today,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Daily_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Daily-33">[33]</a></sup> leading to the gradual <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> of the Sahara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The Sahara is now as dry as it was about 13,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> is the remnant of a former inland sea, paleolake Mega-Chad, which existed during the African humid period. At its largest extent, sometime before 5000 BCE, Lake Mega-Chad was the largest of four Saharan paleolakes, and is estimated to have covered an area of 350,000 km<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a> describes this cycle. During periods of a wet or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Sahara" title="Green Sahara">Green Sahara</a>", the Sahara becomes a <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> grassland and various flora and fauna become more common. Following inter-pluvial arid periods, the Sahara area then reverts to desert conditions and the flora and fauna are forced to retreat northwards to the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a>, southwards into <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, or eastwards into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>. This separates <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">populations</a> of some of the species in areas with different <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a>, forcing them to <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adapt</a>, possibly giving rise to <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric speciation</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_green_wall_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Great_green_wall_map.svg/220px-Great_green_wall_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Great_green_wall_map.svg/330px-Great_green_wall_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Great_green_wall_map.svg/440px-Great_green_wall_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_green_wall_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Green_Wall_(Africa)" title="Great Green Wall (Africa)">Great Green Wall</a>, participating countries and Sahel. In September 2020, it was reported that the GGW had only covered 4% of the planned area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>It is also proposed that humans accelerated the drying-out period from 6000 to 2500 BCE by pastoralists overgrazing available grassland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_for_cycles">Evidence for cycles</span></h4>
<p>The growth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speleothems" title="Speleothems">speleothems</a> (which requires rainwater) was detected in Hol-Zakh, <a href="/wiki/Ashalim" title="Ashalim">Ashalim</a>, Even-Sid, Ma'ale-ha-Meyshar, Ktora Cracks, Nagev Tzavoa Cave, and elsewhere, and has allowed tracking of prehistoric rainfall. The Red Sea coastal route was extremely arid before 140 and after 115 kya (thousands of years ago). Slightly wetter conditions appear at 90–87 kya, but it still was just one tenth the rainfall around 125 kya. In the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negev_Desert" title="Negev Desert">Negev Desert</a> speleothems did not grow between 185 and 140 kya (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_isotope_stage" title="Marine isotope stage">MIS</a> 6), 110–90 (MIS 5.4–5.2), nor after 85 kya nor during most of the interglacial period (MIS 5.1), the glacial period and <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>. This suggests that the southern Negev was arid-to-hyper-arid in these periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaks2007_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaks2007-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> (LGM) the Sahara desert was more extensive than it is now with the extent of the tropical forests being greatly reduced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and the lower temperatures reduced the strength of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadley_Cell" title="Hadley Cell">Hadley Cell</a>. This is a climate cell which causes rising tropical air of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inter-Tropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Inter-Tropical Convergence Zone">Inter-Tropical Convergence Zone</a> (ITCZ) to bring rain to the tropics, while dry descending air, at about <a href="/wiki/20th_parallel_north" title="20th parallel north">20 degrees north</a>, flows back to the equator and brings desert conditions to this region. It is associated with high rates of wind-blown mineral dust, and these dust levels are found as expected in marine cores from the north tropical Atlantic. But around 12,500 BCE the amount of dust in the cores in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%B8lling_Oscillation" title="Bølling Oscillation">Bølling</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aller%C3%B8d_Oscillation" title="Allerød Oscillation">Allerød</a> phase suddenly plummets and shows a period of much wetter conditions in the Sahara, indicating a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dansgaard-Oeschger_event" title="Dansgaard-Oeschger event">Dansgaard-Oeschger</a> (DO) event (a sudden warming followed by a slower cooling of the climate). The moister Saharan conditions had begun about 12,500 BCE, with the extension of the ITCZ northward in the northern hemisphere summer, bringing moist wet conditions and a savanna climate to the Sahara, which (apart from a short dry spell associated with the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">Younger Dryas</a>) peaked during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_thermal_maximum" title="Holocene thermal maximum">Holocene thermal maximum</a> climatic phase at 4000 BCE when mid-latitude temperatures seem to have been between 2 and 3 degrees warmer than in the recent past. Analysis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> deposited sediments in the delta also shows this period had a higher proportion of sediments coming from the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>, suggesting higher rainfall also in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>. This was caused principally by a stronger <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoonal</a> circulation throughout the sub-tropical regions, affecting India, Arabia and the Sahara.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> only recently became the source of the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a> and dried out almost completely around 15 kya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sudden subsequent movement of the ITCZ southwards with a <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_event" title="Heinrich event">Heinrich event</a> (a sudden cooling followed by a slower warming), linked to changes with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">El Niño-Southern Oscillation</a> cycle, led to a rapid drying out of the Saharan and Arabian regions, which quickly became desert. This is linked to a marked decline in the scale of the Nile floods between 2700 and 2100 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecoregions">Ecoregions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="1396" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png/390px-Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png/585px-Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png/780px-Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saharan_topographic_elements_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The major topographic features of the Saharan region</div></div></div>
<p>The Sahara comprises several distinct <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregions</a>. With their variations in temperature, rainfall, elevation, and soil, these regions harbor distinct communities of plants and animals.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_coastal_desert" title="Atlantic coastal desert">Atlantic coastal desert</a> is a narrow strip along the Atlantic coast where fog generated offshore by the cool <a href="/wiki/Canary_Current" title="Canary Current">Canary Current</a> provides sufficient moisture to sustain a variety of <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Succulent_plant" title="Succulent plant">succulents</a>, and shrubs. It covers an area of 39,900 square kilometers (15,400 sq mi) in the south of Morocco and Mauritania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/North_Saharan_steppe_and_woodlands" title="North Saharan steppe and woodlands">North Saharan steppe and woodlands</a> is along the northern desert, next to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_forests,_woodlands,_and_scrub" title="Mediterranean forests, woodlands, and scrub">Mediterranean forests, woodlands, and scrub</a> ecoregions of the northern Maghreb and Cyrenaica. Winter rains sustain shrublands and dry woodlands that form a transition between the Mediterranean climate regions to the north and the hyper-arid Sahara proper to the south. It covers 1,675,300 square kilometers (646,840 sq mi) in Algeria, Egypt, Libya, Mauritania, Morocco, and Tunisia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert_(ecoregion)" title="Sahara Desert (ecoregion)">Sahara Desert ecoregion</a> covers the hyper-arid central portion of the Sahara where rainfall is minimal and sporadic. Vegetation is rare, and this ecoregion consists mostly of sand dunes (<i>erg, chech, raoui</i>), stone plateaus (<i>hamadas</i>), gravel plains (<i>reg</i>), dry valleys (<i>wadis</i>), and salt flats. It covers 4,639,900 square kilometres (1,791,500 sq mi) of: Algeria, Chad, Egypt, Libya, Mali, Mauritania, Niger, and Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA1327_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA1327-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/South_Saharan_steppe_and_woodlands" title="South Saharan steppe and woodlands">South Saharan steppe and woodlands</a> ecoregion is a narrow band running east and west between the hyper-arid Sahara and the Sahel savannas to the south. Movements of the equatorial Intertropical Convergence Zone (ITCZ) bring summer rains during July and August which average 100 to 200 mm (4 to 8 in) but vary greatly from year to year. These rains sustain summer pastures of grasses and herbs, with dry woodlands and shrublands along seasonal watercourses. This ecoregion covers 1,101,700 square kilometres (425,400 sq mi) in Algeria, Chad, Mali, Mauritania, and Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/West_Saharan_montane_xeric_woodlands" title="West Saharan montane xeric woodlands">West Saharan montane xeric woodlands</a>, several volcanic highlands provide a cooler, moister environment that supports Saharo-Mediterranean woodlands and shrublands. The ecoregion covers 258,100 square kilometres (99,650 sq mi), mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Tassili_n%27Ajjer" title="Tassili n'Ajjer">Tassili n'Ajjer</a> of Algeria, with smaller enclaves in the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains" title="Aïr Mountains">Aïr</a> of Niger, the <a href="/wiki/Adrar_Plateau" title="Adrar Plateau">Adrar Plateau</a> of Mauritania, and the <a href="/wiki/Adrar_des_Ifoghas" title="Adrar des Ifoghas">Adrar des Iforas</a> of Mali and Algeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibesti-Jebel_Uweinat_montane_xeric_woodlands" title="Tibesti-Jebel Uweinat montane xeric woodlands">Tibesti-Jebel Uweinat montane xeric woodlands</a> ecoregion consists of the Tibesti and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jebel_Uweinat" title="Jebel Uweinat">Jebel Uweinat</a> highlands. Higher and more regular rainfall and cooler temperatures support woodlands and shrublands of date palm, <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">acacias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myrtus" title="Myrtus">myrtle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oleander" title="Oleander">oleander</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamarix" title="Tamarix">tamarix</a>, and several rare and endemic plants. The ecoregion covers 82,200 square kilometres (31,700 sq mi) in the Tibesti of Chad and Libya, and Jebel Uweinat on the border of Egypt, Libya, and Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Saharan halophytics is an area of seasonally flooded saline depressions which is home to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halophytic" title="Halophytic">halophytic</a> (salt-adapted) plant communities. The Saharan halophytics cover 54,000 square kilometres (21,000 sq mi) including: the <a href="/wiki/Qattara_Depression" title="Qattara Depression">Qattara</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siwa_Depression" title="Siwa Depression">Siwa</a> depressions in northern Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_salt_lakes" title="Tunisian salt lakes">Tunisian salt lakes</a> of central Tunisia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chott_Melghir" title="Chott Melghir">Chott Melghir</a> in Algeria, and smaller areas of Algeria, Mauritania, and the southern part of Morocco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Tanezrouft" title="Tanezrouft">Tanezrouft</a> is one of the Sahara's most arid regions, with no vegetation and very little life. A barren, flat gravel plain, it extends south of <a href="/wiki/Reggane" title="Reggane">Reggane</a> in Algeria towards the <a href="/wiki/Adrar_des_Ifoghas" title="Adrar des Ifoghas">Adrar des Ifoghas</a> highlands in northern Mali.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span></h2>
<p>The flora of the Sahara is highly diversified based on the bio-geographical characteristics of this vast desert. <a href="/wiki/Floristics" title="Floristics">Floristically</a>, the Sahara has three zones based on the amount of rainfall received – the Northern (Mediterranean), Central and Southern Zones. There are two transitional zones – the Mediterranean-Sahara transition and the Sahel transition zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mares1999_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mares1999-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Saharan flora comprises around 2800 species of <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>. Approximately a quarter of these are <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a>. About half of these species are common to the flora of the Arabian deserts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houérou2008_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houérou2008-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GueltaCamels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="763" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/GueltaCamels.jpg/220px-GueltaCamels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/GueltaCamels.jpg/330px-GueltaCamels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/GueltaCamels.jpg/440px-GueltaCamels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GueltaCamels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Camels in the <a href="/wiki/Guelta_d%27Archei" title="Guelta d'Archei">Guelta d'Archei</a>, in north-eastern <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a></div></div></div>
<p>The central Sahara is estimated to include five hundred species of plants, which is extremely low considering the huge extent of the area. Plants such as acacia trees, palms, succulents, spiny shrubs, and grasses have adapted to the arid conditions, by growing lower to avoid water loss by strong winds, by storing water in their thick stems to use it in dry periods, by having long roots that travel horizontally to reach the maximum area of water and to find any surface moisture, and by having small thick leaves or needles to prevent water loss by <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>. Plant leaves may dry out totally and then recover.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Fauna"></span>Several species of fox live in the Sahara including: the <a href="/wiki/Fennec_fox" title="Fennec fox">fennec fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pale_fox" title="Pale fox">pale fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCppell%27s_fox" title="Rüppell's fox">Rüppell's fox</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Addax" title="Addax">addax</a>, a large white <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a>, can go nearly a year in the desert without drinking. The <a href="/wiki/Dorcas_gazelle" title="Dorcas gazelle">dorcas gazelle</a> is a north African gazelle that can also go for a long time without water. Other notable gazelles include the <a href="/wiki/Rhim_gazelle" title="Rhim gazelle">rhim gazelle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dama_gazelle" title="Dama gazelle">dama gazelle</a>.
</p><p>The Saharan <a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Northwest_African_cheetah" title="Northwest African cheetah">northwest African cheetah</a>) lives in Algeria, <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>, Niger, Mali, <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>. There remain fewer than 250 mature cheetahs, which are very cautious, fleeing any human presence. The cheetah avoids the sun from April to October, seeking the shelter of shrubs such as <a href="/wiki/Balanites" title="Balanites">balanites</a> and acacias. They are unusually pale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The other cheetah subspecies (<a href="/wiki/Northeast_African_cheetah" title="Northeast African cheetah">northeast African cheetah</a>) lives in Chad, Sudan and the eastern region of Niger. However, it is currently extinct in the wild in Egypt and Libya. There are approximately 2000 mature individuals left in the wild.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/220px-Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/330px-Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/440px-Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5391_Ubari_Lakes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Idehan_Ubari" title="Idehan Ubari">Idehan Ubari</a> oasis lake, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_plant" title="Native plant">native</a> grasses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenix_dactylifera" title="Phoenix dactylifera">date palms</a></div></div></div>
<p>Other animals include the <a href="/wiki/Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard">monitor lizards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyrax" title="Hyrax">hyrax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerastes_(genus)" title="Cerastes (genus)">sand vipers</a>, and small populations of <a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog" title="African wild dog">African wild dog</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-52">[52]</a></sup> in perhaps only 14 countries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borrell_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borrell-53">[53]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red-necked_ostrich" title="Red-necked ostrich">red-necked ostrich</a>. Other animals exist in the Sahara (birds in particular) such as <a href="/wiki/African_silverbill" title="African silverbill">African silverbill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black-faced_firefinch" title="Black-faced firefinch">black-faced firefinch</a>, among others. There are also small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desert_crocodile" title="Desert crocodile">desert crocodiles</a> in Mauritania and the <a href="/wiki/Ennedi_Plateau" title="Ennedi Plateau">Ennedi Plateau</a> of Chad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Deathstalker" title="Deathstalker">deathstalker</a> scorpion can be 10 cm (3.9 in) long. Its venom contains large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Agitoxin" title="Agitoxin">agitoxin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scyllatoxin" title="Scyllatoxin">scyllatoxin</a> and is very dangerous; however, a sting from this scorpion rarely kills a healthy adult. The <a href="/wiki/Saharan_silver_ant" title="Saharan silver ant">Saharan silver ant</a> is unique in that due to the extreme high temperatures of their habitat, and the threat of predators, the ants are active outside their nest for only about ten minutes per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WehnerEtAl1992_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-WehnerEtAl1992-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dromedary" title="Dromedary">Dromedary</a> camels and <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a> are the domesticated animals most commonly found in the Sahara. Because of its qualities of endurance and speed, the dromedary is the favourite animal used by <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomads</a>.
</p><p>Human activities are more likely to affect the habitat in areas of permanent water (oases) or where water comes close to the surface. Here, the local pressure on natural resources can be intense. The remaining populations of large mammals have been greatly reduced by hunting for food and recreation. In recent years development projects have started in the deserts of Algeria and Tunisia using irrigated water pumped from underground aquifers. These schemes often lead to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" title="Soil degradation">soil degradation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">salinization</a>.
</p><p>Researchers from <a href="/wiki/Hacettepe_University" title="Hacettepe University">Hacettepe University</a> have reported that Saharan soil may have bio-available iron and also some essential macro and micro nutrient elements suitable for use as fertilizer for growing wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YTSB2011_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-YTSB2011-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mathendous_giraffes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mathendous_giraffes.jpg/220px-Mathendous_giraffes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mathendous_giraffes.jpg/330px-Mathendous_giraffes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mathendous_giraffes.jpg/440px-Mathendous_giraffes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mathendous_giraffes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saharan_rock_art" title="Saharan rock art">Saharan rock art</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Fezzan" title="Fezzan">Fezzan</a></div></div></div>
<p>People lived on the edge of the desert thousands of years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Post-Ice_Age_Sahara_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Post-Ice_Age_Sahara-57">[57]</a></sup> since the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>. In the Central Sahara, engraved and painted rock art were created perhaps as early as 10,000 years ago, spanning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bubaline_Period" title="Bubaline Period">Bubaline Period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kel_Essuf_Period" title="Kel Essuf Period">Kel Essuf Period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Round_Head_Period" title="Round Head Period">Round Head Period</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_Period" title="Pastoral Period">Pastoral Period</a>, Caballine Period, and Cameline Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soukopova_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soukopova-58">[58]</a></sup> The Sahara was then a much wetter place than it is today. Over 30,000 <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphs</a> of river animals such as <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crocodiles_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crocodiles-59">[59]</a></sup> survive, with half found in the Tassili n'Ajjer in southeast Algeria. <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">Fossils</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2018_Dinosaur_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-2018_Dinosaur-60">[60]</a></sup> including <i><a href="/wiki/Afrovenator" title="Afrovenator">Afrovenator</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jobaria" title="Jobaria">Jobaria</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ouranosaurus" title="Ouranosaurus">Ouranosaurus</a></i>, have also been found here. The modern Sahara, though, is not lush in vegetation, except in the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> Valley, at a few <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oases</a>, and in the northern highlands, where Mediterranean plants such as the <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olive</a> tree are found to grow. It was long believed that the region had been this way since about 1600 BCE, after shifts in <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">Earth's axis</a> increased temperatures and decreased precipitation, which led to the abrupt desertification of North Africa about 5,400 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Daily_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Daily-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kiffians">Kiffians</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kiffian_culture" title="Kiffian culture">Kiffian culture</a> is a prehistoric industry, or domain, that existed between 10,000 and 8,000 years ago in the Sahara, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_Subpluvial" title="Neolithic Subpluvial">Neolithic Subpluvial</a>. Human remains from this culture were found in 2000 at a site known as <a href="/wiki/Gobero" title="Gobero">Gobero</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> in the <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9n%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Ténéré">Ténéré Desert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scidaily_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-scidaily-61">[61]</a></sup> The site is known as the largest and earliest grave of Stone Age people in the Sahara desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-62">[62]</a></sup> The Kiffians were skilled <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunters</a>. Bones of many large savannah animals that were discovered in the same area suggest that they lived on the shores of a lake that was present during the Holocene Wet Phase, a period when the Sahara was verdant and wet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-62">[62]</a></sup> The Kiffian people were tall, standing over six feet in height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scidaily_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-scidaily-61">[61]</a></sup> Craniometric analysis indicates that this early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> population was closely related to the Late Pleistocene <a href="/wiki/Iberomaurusian" title="Iberomaurusian">Iberomaurusians</a> and early Holocene <a href="/wiki/Capsian_culture" title="Capsian culture">Capsians</a> of the Maghreb, as well as mid-Holocene <a href="/wiki/Mechta-Afalou" title="Mechta-Afalou">Mechta</a> groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Traces of the Kiffian culture do not exist after 8,000 years ago, as the Sahara went through a dry period for the next thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientist_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-newscientist-64">[64]</a></sup> After this time, the <a href="/wiki/Tenerian_culture" title="Tenerian culture">Tenerian culture</a> colonized the area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenerians">Tenerians</span></h3>
<p>Gobero was discovered in 2000 during an archaeological expedition led by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Sereno" title="Paul Sereno">Paul Sereno</a>, which sought <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaur</a> remains. Two distinct prehistoric cultures were discovered at the site: the early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> Kiffian culture, and the middle Holocene <a href="/wiki/Tenerian_culture" title="Tenerian culture">Tenerian culture</a>. The post-Kiffian desiccation lasted until around 4600 BCE, when the earliest artefacts associated with the Tenerians have been dated to. Some 200 skeletons have been discovered at Gobero. The Tenerians were considerably shorter in height and less robust than the earlier Kiffians. Craniometric analysis also indicates that they were osteologically distinct. The Kiffian skulls are akin to those of the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Iberomaurusian" title="Iberomaurusian">Iberomaurusians</a>, early Holocene <a href="/wiki/Capsian_culture" title="Capsian culture">Capsians</a>, and mid-Holocene <a href="/wiki/Mechta-Afalou" title="Mechta-Afalou">Mechta</a> groups, whereas the Tenerian crania are more like those of Mediterranean groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Graves show that the Tenerians observed spiritual traditions, as they were buried with artifacts such as jewelry made of hippo <a href="/wiki/Tusk" title="Tusk">tusks</a> and clay pots. The most interesting find is a triple burial, dated to 5300 years ago, of an adult female and two children, estimated through their teeth as being five and eight years old, hugging each other. Pollen residue indicates they were buried on a bed of flowers. The three are assumed to have died within 24 hours of each other, but as their skeletons hold no apparent trauma (they did not die violently) and they have been buried so elaborately – unlikely if they had died of a plague – the cause of their deaths is a mystery.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vue_de_Taghit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Vue_de_Taghit.jpg/220px-Vue_de_Taghit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Vue_de_Taghit.jpg/330px-Vue_de_Taghit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Vue_de_Taghit.jpg/440px-Vue_de_Taghit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vue_de_Taghit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oued_Zouzfana" title="Oued Zouzfana">Oued Zouzfana</a> and village of <a href="/wiki/Taghit" title="Taghit">Taghit</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tashwinat_Mummy">Tashwinat Mummy</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uan_Muhuggiag" title="Uan Muhuggiag">Uan Muhuggiag</a> appears to have been inhabited from at least the 6th millennium BCE to about 2700 BCE, although not necessarily continuously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-67">[67]</a></sup> The most noteworthy find at Uan Muhuggiag is the well-preserved <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">mummy</a> of a young boy of approximately <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> years old. The child was in a fetal position, then embalmed, then placed in a sack made of antelope skin, which was insulated by a layer of leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-68">[68]</a></sup> The boy's organs were removed, as evidenced by incisions in his stomach and thorax, and an organic preservative was inserted to stop his body from decomposing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-69">[69]</a></sup> An ostrich eggshell necklace was also found around his neck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> determined the age of the mummy to be approximately 5600 years old, which makes it about 1000 years older than the earliest previously recorded mummy in ancient <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-70">[70]</a></sup> In 1958–59, an archaeological expedition led by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Antonio_Ascenzi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Antonio Ascenzi (page does not exist)">Antonio Ascenzi</a> conducted anthropological, radiological, histological and chemical analyses on the Uan Muhuggiag mummy.
