The team claimed that the mummy was a 30-month-old child of uncertain sex who possessed <a href="/wiki/Negroid" title="Negroid">Negroid</a> features (Note: "negroid" is derived from a disproven theory of biological race, and modern genetics has since rendered such terminology obsolete).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> A long incision on the specimen's abdominal wall also indicated that the body had been initially mummified by evisceration and later underwent natural desiccation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giuffra2010_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giuffra2010-73">[73]</a></sup> One other individual, an adult, was found at Uan Muhuggiag, buried in a crouched position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-67">[67]</a></sup> However, the body showed no evidence of evisceration or any other method of preservation. The body was estimated to date from about 7500 BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nubians">Nubians</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_Subpluvial" title="Neolithic Subpluvial">Neolithic Subpluvial</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beni-Izguen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Beni-Izguen.jpg/220px-Beni-Izguen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Beni-Izguen.jpg/330px-Beni-Izguen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Beni-Izguen.jpg/440px-Beni-Izguen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beni-Izguen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/M%27zab" title="M'zab">Beni Isguen</a>, a holy city surrounded by thick walls in the Algerian Sahara</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> Era, before the onset of <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> around 9500 BCE, the central Sudan had been a rich environment supporting a large population ranging across what is now barren desert, like the Wadi el-Qa'ab. By the 5th millennium BCE, the people who inhabited what is now called <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> were full participants in the "agricultural revolution", living a settled lifestyle with domesticated plants and animals. Saharan rock art of cattle and herdsmen suggests the presence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattle_cult" title="Cattle cult">cattle cult</a> like those found in Sudan and other pastoral societies in Africa today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">Megaliths</a> found at <a href="/wiki/Nabta_Playa" title="Nabta Playa">Nabta Playa</a> are overt examples of probably the world's first known <a href="/wiki/Archaeoastronomy" title="Archaeoastronomy">archaeoastronomy</a> devices, predating <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> by some 2,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This complexity, as observed at Nabta Playa, and as expressed by different levels of authority within the society there, likely formed the basis for the structure of both the Neolithic society at Nabta and the Old Kingdom of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
Archaeological evidence has attested that population settlements occurred in Nubia as early as the Late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> era and from the 5th millennium BC onwards, whereas there is "no or scanty evidence" of human presence in the Egyptian Nile Valley during these periods, which may be due to problems in site preservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egyptians">Egyptians</span></h3>
<p>By 6000 BCE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic Egyptians</a> in the southwestern corner of Egypt were herding cattle and constructing large buildings. Subsistence in organized and permanent settlements in predynastic Egypt by the middle of the 6th millennium BCE centered predominantly on cereal and animal agriculture: cattle, goats, pigs and sheep. Metal objects replaced prior ones of stone. Tanning of animal skins, pottery and weaving were commonplace in this era also. There are indications of seasonal or only temporary occupation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Fayyum" title="Al Fayyum">Al Fayyum</a> in the 6th millennium BCE, with food activities centering on fishing, hunting and food-gathering. Stone <a href="/wiki/Arrowhead" title="Arrowhead">arrowheads</a>, knives and scrapers from the era are commonly found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fayum_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fayum-79">[79]</a></sup> Burial items included pottery, jewelry, farming and hunting equipment, and assorted foods including dried meat and fruit. Burial in desert environments appears to enhance Egyptian preservation rites, and the dead were buried facing due west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Predynastic_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Predynastic-80">[80]</a></sup> Several scholars have argued that the African origins of the Egyptian civilisation derived from pastoral communities which emerged in both the Egyptian and Sudanese regions of the Nile Valley in the fifth millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 3400 BCE, the Sahara was as dry as it is today, due to reduced precipitation and higher temperatures resulting from a shift in Earth's orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Daily_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Daily-33">[33]</a></sup> As a result of this aridification, it became a largely impenetrable barrier to humans, with the remaining settlements mainly being concentrated around the numerous oases that dot the landscape. Little trade or commerce is known to have passed through the interior in subsequent periods, the only major exception being the Nile Valley. The Nile, however, was impassable at several <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">cataracts</a>, making trade and contact by boat difficult.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tichitt_culture">Tichitt culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tichitt_culture" title="Tichitt culture">Tichitt culture</a></div>
<p>In 4000 BCE, the start of sophisticated social structure (e.g., trade of cattle as valued assets) developed among herders amid the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_Period" title="Pastoral Period">Pastoral Period</a> of the Sahara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brass3_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brass3-82">[82]</a></sup> Saharan <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">pastoral</a> culture (e.g., fields of tumuli, lustrous stone rings, axes) was intricate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brass_II_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brass_II-83">[83]</a></sup> By 1800 BCE, Saharan pastoral culture expanded throughout the Saharan and Sahelian regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brass3_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brass3-82">[82]</a></sup> The initial stages of sophisticated social structure among Saharan herders served as the segue for the development of sophisticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierarchies" title="Hierarchies">hierarchies</a> found in African settlements, such as <a href="/wiki/Dhar_Tichitt" title="Dhar Tichitt">Dhar Tichitt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brass3_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brass3-82">[82]</a></sup> After migrating from the Central Sahara, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mande_peoples" title="Mande peoples">Mande peoples</a> established their civilization in the Tichitt region<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abd-El-Moniem_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abd-El-Moniem-84">[84]</a></sup> of the Western Sahara<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kea_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kea-85">[85]</a></sup> The Tichitt Tradition of eastern Mauritania dates from 2200 BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDougall_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDougall-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-87">[87]</a></sup> to 200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_IV_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_IV-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kay-89">[89]</a></sup> Tichitt culture, at Dhar Néma, Dhar Tagant, Dhar Tichitt, and Dhar Walata, included a four-tiered hierarchal social structure, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farming" title="Farming">farming</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cereals" title="Cereals">cereals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Funerary" title="Funerary">funerary</a> tombs, and a <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a> tradition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sterry_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sterry-90">[90]</a></sup> At Dhar Tichitt and Dhar Walata, <a href="/wiki/Pearl_millet" title="Pearl millet">pearl millet</a> may have also been independently tamed amid the <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_Neolithic" title="Pastoral Neolithic">Neolithic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Champion_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Champion-91">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kea_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kea-85">[85]</a></sup> Tichitt Tradition may have been the earliest large-scale, <a href="/wiki/Complex_society" title="Complex society">complexly organized society</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_II_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_II-92">[92]</a></sup> and an early <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> of the Sahara,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abd-El-Moniem_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abd-El-Moniem-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDougall_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-McDougall-86">[86]</a></sup> which may have served as the segue for <a href="/wiki/State_formation" title="State formation">state formation</a> in West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brass_II_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brass_II-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>As areas where the Tichitt cultural tradition were present, Dhar Tichitt and Dhar Walata were occupied more frequently than Dhar Néma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_II_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_II-92">[92]</a></sup> Farming of crops (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>) may have been a feature of the Tichitt cultural tradition as early as 3rd millennium BCE in Dhar Tichitt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_II_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_II-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of a broader trend of iron metallurgy developed in the West African Sahel amid 1st millennium BCE, iron items (350 BCE – 100 CE) were found at Dhar Tagant, iron metalworking and/or items (800 BCE – 400 BCE) were found at <a href="/wiki/Dia,_Mali" title="Dia, Mali">Dia Shoma</a> and Walaldé, and the iron remnants (760 BCE – 400 BCE) found at Bou Khzama and Djiganyai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_II_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_II-92">[92]</a></sup> The iron materials that were found are evidence of iron metalworking at Dhar Tagant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kay-89">[89]</a></sup> In the late period of the Tichitt Tradition at Dhar Néma, tamed pearl millet was used to temper the tuyeres of a oval-shaped low shaft furnace; this furnace was one out of 16 iron furnaces located on elevated ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_IV_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_IV-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iron_metallurgy_in_Africa" title="Iron metallurgy in Africa">Iron metallurgy</a> may have developed before the second half of 1st millennium BCE, as indicated by pottery dated between 800 BCE and 200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_IV_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald_IV-88">[88]</a></sup> At Dhar Walata and Dhar Tichitt, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> was also utilized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kea_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kea-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>After its decline in Mauritania, the Tichitt Tradition spread to the Middle Niger region (e.g., <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9ma" title="Méma">Méma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macina,_Mali" title="Macina, Mali">Macina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dia,_Mali" title="Dia, Mali">Dia Shoma</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jenne_Jeno" title="Jenne Jeno">Jenne Jeno</a>) of Mali where it developed into and persisted as Faïta Facies ceramics between 1300 BCE and 400 BCE among <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> architecture and iron metallurgy (which had developed after 900 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald2_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald2-93">[93]</a></sup> Thereafter, the <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a> developed in the 1st millennium CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald2_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald2-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_Sahara" title="History of Western Sahara">History of Western Sahara</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg/220px-Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg/330px-Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg/440px-Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bilma-Salzkarawane1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Azalai" title="Azalai">Azalai</a> salt caravan. The French reported that the 1906 caravan numbered 20,000 camels.</div></div></div>
<p>The people of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, who flourished from 1200 to 800 BCE, created a chain of settlements along the coast of North Africa and traded extensively with its inhabitants. This put them in contact with the people of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">ancient Libya</a>, who were the ancestors of people who speak <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber languages</a> in North Africa and the Sahara today.
</p><p>The Libyco-Berber alphabet of the ancient Libyans of north Africa seems to have been based on Phoenician, and its descendant <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh</a> is still used today by the (Berber) <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> of the central Sahara.
</p><p>The Periplus of the Phoenician navigator <a href="/wiki/Hanno_the_Navigator" title="Hanno the Navigator">Hanno</a>, who lived sometime in the 5th century BC, claims that he founded settlements along the Atlantic coast of Africa, possibly including the Western Sahara. The identification of the places discussed is controversial, and archeological confirmation is lacking.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greeks">Greeks</span></h3>
<p>By 500 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> arrived in the desert. Greek traders spread along the eastern coast of the desert, establishing trading colonies along the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginians</a> explored the Atlantic coast of the desert, but the turbulence of the waters and the lack of markets caused a lack of presence further south than modern Morocco. Centralized states thus surrounded the desert on the north and east; it remained outside the control of these states. Raids from the nomadic Berber people of the desert were of constant concern to those living on the edge of the desert.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_civilization">Urban civilization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghardaia01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2196" data-file-width="3375" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ghardaia01.jpg/220px-Ghardaia01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ghardaia01.jpg/330px-Ghardaia01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ghardaia01.jpg/440px-Ghardaia01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghardaia01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Market on the main square of <a href="/wiki/Gharda%C3%AFa" title="Ghardaïa">Ghardaïa</a> (1971)</div></div></div>
<p>An urban civilization, the <a href="/wiki/Garamantes" title="Garamantes">Garamantes</a>, arose around 500 BCE in the heart of the Sahara, in a valley that is now called the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wadi_al-Ajal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wadi al-Ajal (page does not exist)">Wadi al-Ajal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fezzan" title="Fezzan">Fezzan</a>, Libya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes-27">[27]</a></sup> The Garamantes achieved this development by digging tunnels far into the mountains flanking the valley to tap <a href="/wiki/Fossil_water" title="Fossil water">fossil water</a> and bring it to their fields. The Garamantes grew populous and strong, conquering their neighbors and capturing many slaves (who were put to work extending the tunnels). The ancient Greeks and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> knew of the Garamantes and regarded them as uncivilized nomads. However, they traded with them, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_bath" title="Roman bath">Roman bath</a> has been found in the Garamantes' capital of Garama. <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeologists</a> have found eight major towns and many other important settlements in the Garamantes' territory. The Garamantes' civilization eventually collapsed after they had depleted available water in the <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a> and could no longer sustain the effort to extend the tunnels further into the mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keys_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keys-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between the first century BCE and the fourth century CE, several <a href="/wiki/Romans_in_Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Romans in Sub-Saharan Africa">Roman expeditions into the Sahara</a> were conducted by groups of military and commercial units of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Berbers">Berbers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG/220px-Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG/330px-Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG/440px-Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marabutto-Taghirt.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zawiya_(institution)" title="Zawiya (institution)">Zawiya</a> at the entrance of <a href="/wiki/Taghit" title="Taghit">Taghit</a>, Algeria</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_caravane2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1390" data-file-width="1040" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hoggar_caravane2.jpg/170px-Hoggar_caravane2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hoggar_caravane2.jpg/255px-Hoggar_caravane2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hoggar_caravane2.jpg/340px-Hoggar_caravane2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_caravane2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> once controlled the central Sahara and its trade.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber people</a> occupied (and still occupy with Arabs) much of the Sahara. The Garamantes Berbers built a prosperous empire in the heart of the desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The Tuareg nomads continue to inhabit and move across wide Sahara surfaces to the present day.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_and_Arabic_expansion">Islamic and Arabic expansion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trade</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamization_of_Sudan" title="Islamization of Sudan">Islamization of Sudan</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> ruled the northern shores of the Sahara from the 5th to the 7th centuries. After the Muslim conquest of Arabia, specifically the Arabian peninsula, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_North_Africa" title="Muslim conquest of North Africa">Muslim conquest of North Africa</a> began in the mid-7th to early 8th centuries and Islamic influence expanded rapidly on the Sahara. By the end of 641 all of Egypt was in Muslim hands. Trade across the desert intensified, and a significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> crossed the desert. It has been estimated that from the 10th to 19th centuries some 6,000 to 7,000 slaves were transported north each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Beni_%E1%B8%A4ass%C4%81n" title="Beni Ḥassān">Beni Ḥassān</a> and other nomadic Arab tribes dominated the <a href="/wiki/Sanhaja" title="Sanhaja">Sanhaja</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> tribes of the western Sahara after the <a href="/wiki/Char_Bouba_war" title="Char Bouba war">Char Bouba war</a> of the 17th century. As a result, Arabian culture and language came to dominate, and the Berber tribes underwent some <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabization</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Turkish_era">Ottoman Turkish era</span></h3>
<p>In the 16th century the northern fringe of the Sahara, such as coastal regencies in present-day <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Algeria" title="Ottoman Algeria">Algeria</a> and Tunisia, as well as some parts of present-day Libya, together with the semi-autonomous kingdom of Egypt, were occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. From 1517 Egypt was a valued part of the Ottoman Empire, ownership of which provided the Ottomans with control over the Nile Valley, the east Mediterranean and North Africa. The benefit of the Ottoman Empire was the freedom of movement for citizens and goods. Traders exploited the Ottoman land routes to handle the spices, gold and silk from the East, manufactured goods from Europe, and the slave and gold traffic from Africa. Arabic continued as the local language and Islamic culture was much reinforced. The Sahel and southern Sahara regions were home to several independent states or to roaming Tuareg clans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_colonialism">European colonialism</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonialism" title="European colonialism">European colonialism</a> in the Sahara began in the 19th century. <a href="/wiki/July_Monarchy" title="July Monarchy">France</a> conquered the regency of Algiers from the Ottomans in 1830, and French rule spread south from <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">French Algeria</a> and eastwards from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Senegal" title="French Senegal">Senegal</a> into the upper <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger</a> to include present-day Algeria, Chad, Mali then <a href="/wiki/French_Sudan" title="French Sudan">French Sudan</a> including <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a> (1893), Mauritania, Morocco (1912), Niger, and Tunisia (1881). By the beginning of the 20th century, the trans-Saharan trade had clearly declined because goods were moved through more modern and efficient means, such as airplanes, rather than across the desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The French took advantage of long-standing animosity between the <a href="/wiki/Chaamba" title="Chaamba">Chaamba</a> Arabs and the Tuareg. The newly raised <i>Méhariste</i> <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9hariste" title="Méhariste">camel corps</a> were originally recruited mainly from the Chaamba nomadic tribe. In 1902, the French penetrated <a href="/wiki/Hoggar_Mountains" title="Hoggar Mountains">Hoggar Mountains</a> and defeated <a href="/wiki/Kel_Ahaggar" title="Kel Ahaggar">Ahaggar Tuareg</a> in the battle of <a href="/wiki/Tit,_Tamanrasset" title="Tit, Tamanrasset">Tit</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1142" data-file-width="2311" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png/220px-Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png/330px-Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png/440px-Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a> (blue) was the dominant presence in the Sahara</div></div></div>
<p>The French Colonial Empire was the dominant presence in the Sahara. It established regular air links from <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a> (HQ of famed <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9ropostale_(aviation)" title="Aéropostale (aviation)">Aéropostale</a>), to <a href="/wiki/Oran" title="Oran">Oran</a> and over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoggar" title="Hoggar">Hoggar</a> to Timbuktu and West to <a href="/wiki/Bamako" title="Bamako">Bamako</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a>, as well as trans-Sahara bus services run by La Compagnie Transsaharienne (est. 1927).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wauthier1_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-wauthier1-98">[98]</a></sup> A remarkable film shot by famous aviator Captain <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Wauthier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="René Wauthier (page does not exist)">René Wauthier</a> documents the first crossing by a large truck convoy from Algiers to Tchad, across the Sahara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reconnaissance_saharienne_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reconnaissance_saharienne-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt, under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a> and his successors, conquered Nubia in 1820–22, founded <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> in 1823, and conquered <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> in 1874. Egypt, including the Sudan, became a British protectorate in 1882. Egypt and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> lost control of the Sudan from 1882 to 1898 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">Mahdist War</a>. After its capture by British troops in 1898, the Sudan became an Anglo-Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Condominium_(international_law)" title="Condominium (international law)">condominium</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Spain)" title="Restoration (Spain)">Spain</a> captured present-day <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> after 1874, although Rio del Oro remained largely under <a href="/wiki/Sahrawi_people" title="Sahrawi people">Sahrawi</a> influence. In 1912, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italy</a> captured parts of what was to be named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Libya" title="Ottoman Libya">Libya</a> from the Ottomans. To promote the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> religion in the desert, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a> appointed a delegate Apostolic of the Sahara and the Sudan in 1868; later in the 19th century his jurisdiction was reorganized into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vicariate_Apostolic_of_Sahara" title="Vicariate Apostolic of Sahara">Vicariate Apostolic of Sahara</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakup_of_the_empires_and_afterwards">Breakup of the empires and afterwards</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/220px-Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/330px-Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/440px-Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5101_Fozzigiaren_Arch_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A natural rock arch in south western Libya</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_Desert_(Algerie).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg/220px-Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg/330px-Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Hoggar_Desert_%28Algerie%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoggar_Desert_(Algerie).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sahara today</div></div></div>
<p>Egypt became independent of Britain in 1936, although the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_treaty_of_1936" title="Anglo-Egyptian treaty of 1936">Anglo-Egyptian treaty of 1936</a> allowed Britain to keep troops in Egypt and to maintain the British-Egyptian condominium in the Sudan. British military forces were withdrawn in 1954.
</p><p>Most of the Saharan states achieved independence after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>: Libya in 1951; Morocco, Sudan, and Tunisia in 1956; Chad, Mali, Mauritania, and Niger in 1960; and Algeria in 1962. Spain withdrew from <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> in 1975, and it was partitioned between Mauritania and Morocco. Mauritania withdrew in 1979; Morocco continues to hold the territory (see <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara_conflict" title="Western Sahara conflict">Western Sahara conflict</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg people</a> in Mali <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Tuareg_rebellions_(disambiguation)" title="Tuareg rebellions (disambiguation)">rebelled</a> several times during the 20th century before finally forcing the Malian armed forces to withdraw below the line demarcating <a href="/wiki/Azawad" title="Azawad">Azawad</a> from southern Mali during the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_rebellion_(2012)" title="Tuareg rebellion (2012)">2012 rebellion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Islamist rebels in the Sahara calling themselves <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Islamic_Maghreb" title="Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb">al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb</a> have stepped up their violence in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the post–World War II era, several <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a> and communities have developed to utilize the desert's natural resources. These include large deposits of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> in Algeria and Libya, and large deposits of <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a> in Morocco and Western Sahara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Libya's <a href="/wiki/Great_Man-Made_River" title="Great Man-Made River">Great Man-Made River</a> is the world's largest irrigation project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The project utilizes a pipeline system that pumps <a href="/wiki/Fossil_water" title="Fossil water">fossil water</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Sandstone_Aquifer_System" title="Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System">Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System</a> to cities in the populous Libyan northern Mediterranean coast including Tripoli and Benghazi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Trans-African_Highway_network" title="Trans-African Highway network">Trans-African highways</a> have been proposed across the Sahara, including the <a href="/wiki/Cairo%E2%80%93Dakar_Highway" title="Cairo–Dakar Highway">Cairo–Dakar Highway</a> along the Atlantic coast, the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Sahara_Highway" title="Trans-Sahara Highway">Trans-Sahara Highway</a> from <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> on the Mediterranean to <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a> in Nigeria, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tripoli_%E2%80%93_Cape_Town_Highway" title="Tripoli – Cape Town Highway">Tripoli – Cape Town Highway</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a> in Libya to <a href="/wiki/N%27Djamena" title="N'Djamena">N'Djamena</a> in Chad, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cairo_%E2%80%93_Cape_Town_Highway" title="Cairo – Cape Town Highway">Cairo – Cape Town Highway</a> which follows the Nile. Each of these highways is partially complete, with significant gaps and unpaved sections.
</p>
<h2><span id="People.2C_culture.2C_and_languages"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="People,_culture,_and_languages">People, culture, and languages</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Saharan_languages" title="Saharan languages">Saharan languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saharan_rock_art" title="Saharan rock art">Saharan rock art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rock_art#Sahara" title="Rock art">Rock art § Sahara</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabslavers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="556" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arabslavers.jpg/220px-Arabslavers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arabslavers.jpg/330px-Arabslavers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Arabslavers.jpg/440px-Arabslavers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabslavers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 19th-century engraving of an Arab slave-trading caravan transporting black African slaves across the Sahara</div></div></div>
<p>The people of the Sahara are of various origins. Among them the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazigh" title="Amazigh">Amazigh</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a>, various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabized" title="Arabized">Arabized</a> Amaziɣ groups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hassaniya" title="Hassaniya">Hassaniya</a>-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahrawis" title="Sahrawis">Sahrawis</a>, whose populations include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Znaga" title="Znaga">Znaga</a>, a tribe whose name is a remnant of the pre-historic <a href="/wiki/Zenaga_language" title="Zenaga language">Zenaga language</a>. Other major groups of people include the: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toubou" title="Toubou">Toubou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">Nubians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songhai_people" title="Songhai people">Songhai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula/Fulani</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Peul</i>; <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula</a>: <i lang="ff-Latn">Fulɓe</i>). The archaeological evidence from the Holocene period has shown that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan" title="Nilo-Saharan">Nilo-Saharan</a> speaking groups had populated the central and southern Sahara before the influx of <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> speakers, around 1500 years ago, who now largely populate the Sahara in the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_dialects" title="Arabic dialects">Arabic dialects</a> are the most widely spoken languages in the Sahara. Arabic, Berber and its variants now regrouped under the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazigh" title="Amazigh">Amazigh</a> (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Guanche_language" title="Guanche language">Guanche language</a> spoken by the original Berber inhabitants of the Canary Islands) and Beja languages are part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic" title="Afro-Asiatic">Afro-Asiatic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamito-Semitic" title="Hamito-Semitic">Hamito-Semitic</a> family.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Unlike neighboring <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> and the central governments of the states that comprise the Sahara, the French language bears little relevance to inter-personal discourse and commerce within the region, its people retaining staunch ethnic and political affiliations with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuareg" title="Tuareg">Tuareg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> leaders and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The legacy of the French <a href="/wiki/French_North_Africa" title="French North Africa">colonial era</a> administration is primarily manifested in the territorial reorganization enacted by the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic" title="French Fourth Republic">Fourth</a> republics, which engendered artificial political divisions within a hitherto isolated and porous region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Diplomacy with local clients was conducted primarily in Arabic, which was the traditional language of bureaucratic affairs. Mediation of disputes and inter-agency communication was served by interpreters contracted by the French government, who, according to Keenan, "documented a space of intercultural mediation," contributing much to preserving the indigenous cultural identities in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
